Example 5: JobPost Analytics Advanced Manifest Management

Implement advanced manifest management for JobPost analytics service including version control, rollback capabilities, and A/B testing across different platform configurations for machine learning workloads.

Create versioned JobPost analytics manifests:

# Create baseline analytics manifest
docker buildx imagetools create \
    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:baseline-v4.0 \
    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:stable \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:ml-v4.0

# Create experimental ML variant
docker buildx build --platform linux/amd64,linux/arm64 \
    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1 \
    --build-arg TENSORFLOW_VERSION=2.14.0 \
    --build-arg EXPERIMENTAL_FEATURES=true \
    --push -f Dockerfile.jobpost-analytics-experimental .

# Create A/B testing manifest combining versions
docker buildx imagetools create \
    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:ab-test-v4 \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:baseline-v4.0@$(docker buildx imagetools inspect anihpj/jobpost-analytics:baseline-v4.0 --format '{{.Digest}}') \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1@$(docker buildx imagetools inspect anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1 --format '{{.Digest}}')
[Experimental Build] [+] Building 95.3s (42/42) FINISHED => pushing manifest for docker.io/anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1 0.4s [A/B Manifest Creation] anihpj/jobpost-analytics:ab-test-v4

Implement version control and rollback strategy:

# Create version manifest tracking
cat > analytics-versions.json << 'EOF'
{
  "versions": {
    "v4.0": {
      "manifest": "anihpj/jobpost-analytics:baseline-v4.0",
      "digest": "sha256:baseline123...",
      "features": ["standard_ml", "basic_analytics"],
      "stability": "stable"
    },
    "v4.1": {
      "manifest": "anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1", 
      "digest": "sha256:experimental456...",
      "features": ["advanced_ml", "experimental_analytics", "tensorflow_2.14"],
      "stability": "experimental"
    }
  }
}
EOF

# Function to rollback to specific version
rollback_analytics() {
    VERSION=$1
    MANIFEST=$(jq -r ".versions.\"$VERSION\".manifest" analytics-versions.json)
    docker buildx imagetools create -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:current $MANIFEST
    echo "Rolled back to $VERSION: $MANIFEST"
}

Deploy A/B testing configuration:

# Deploy baseline version (Control)
docker run -d -p 8095:8095 --name analytics-control \
    -e VARIANT=baseline \
    -e ML_VERSION=v4.0 \
    -e ANALYTICS_MODE=control \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:baseline-v4.0

# Deploy experimental version (Test)  
docker run -d -p 8096:8095 --name analytics-test \
    -e VARIANT=experimental \
    -e ML_VERSION=v4.1 \
    -e ANALYTICS_MODE=test \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1

Test A/B performance comparison:

# Test baseline analytics performance
curl -X POST "http://localhost:8095/api/analytics/predict-job-match/" \
    -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
    -d '{
      "skills": ["Python", "Django", "Machine Learning"],
      "experience": 5,
      "location_preference": "remote",
      "salary_expectation": 150000
    }' | jq '.performance'

# Test experimental analytics performance  
curl -X POST "http://localhost:8096/api/analytics/predict-job-match/" \
    -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
    -d '{
      "skills": ["Python", "Django", "Machine Learning"],
      "experience": 5,
      "location_preference": "remote", 
      "salary_expectation": 150000
    }' | jq '.performance'
# Baseline Performance (v4.0): { "processing_time_ms": 456, "model_version": "v4.0", "tensorflow_version": "2.13.0", "accuracy_score": 0.87, "variant": "baseline", "manifest_type": "stable" } # Experimental Performance (v4.1): { "processing_time_ms": 298, "model_version": "v4.1", "tensorflow_version": "2.14.0", "accuracy_score": 0.91, "variant": "experimental", "manifest_type": "experimental", "improvement": "35% faster, 4.6% more accurate" }

Analyze manifest performance and make deployment decision:

# Collect A/B test metrics
echo "=== Performance Comparison ==="
echo "Baseline: 456ms, 87% accuracy"
echo "Experimental: 298ms, 91% accuracy"
echo "Improvement: 35% faster, 4.6% more accurate"

# Promote experimental to production based on results
docker buildx imagetools create \
    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:production-v4.1 \
    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:latest \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:experimental-v4.1

# Create rollback manifest for safety

    -t anihpj/jobpost-analytics:rollback-v4.0 \
    anihpj/jobpost-analytics:baseline-v4.0
=== Performance Comparison === Baseline: 456ms, 87% accuracy Experimental: 298ms, 91% accuracy Improvement: 35% faster, 4.6% more accurate anihpj/jobpost-analytics:production-v4.1 anihpj/jobpost-analytics:latest anihpj/jobpost-analytics:rollback-v4.0

# Deploy promoted production analytics

# Test JobPost model with advanced manifest management

from jobpost.models import JobPost, Skills, Author, Location, User
from django.db.models import Count, Avg, Q, F
import os
import time

# Advanced analytics deployment analysis
print("JobPost Analytics - Advanced Manifest Management")
print(f"Deployment Version: {os.getenv('ML_VERSION', 'v4.1')}")
print(f"Variant: {os.getenv('VARIANT', 'production')}")
print(f"Analytics Mode: {os.getenv('ANALYTICS_MODE', 'production')}")

# Advanced ML-powered JobPost analytics
start = time.time()
ml_analytics = JobPost.objects.select_related('location', 'author').prefetch_related('skills').annotate(
    skills_count=Count('skills'),
    skill_demand_avg=Avg('skills__demand_score'),
    location_density=Count('location__jobpost')
).filter(
    salary_max__isnull=False,
    skills_count__gte=2
)

analytics_time = time.time() - start
print(f"Advanced Analytics Query: {analytics_time:.4f}s")

# ML-enhanced job matching analytics
high_value_jobs = ml_analytics.filter(
    skill_demand_avg__gte=85,
    salary_max__gte=120000
).order_by('-skill_demand_avg', '-salary_max')

print("High-Value Job Analytics (ML v4.1):")
for job in high_value_jobs[:5]:
    print(f"  {job.title}: {job.skill_demand_avg:.1f} avg demand, ${job.salary_max:,}")

# Manifest deployment metrics
deployment_metrics = {
    'total_jobs_analyzed': ml_analytics.count(),
    'high_value_jobs': high_value_jobs.count(),
    'ml_version': 'v4.1',
    'performance_improvement': '35% faster than v4.0',
    'accuracy_improvement': '4.6% better predictions'
}

print("Advanced Analytics Metrics:", deployment_metrics)

docker build (legacy builder)

Official Docs: docker build | Docker CLI: docker build --disable-buildkit

Build Docker images using the legacy Docker builder (BuildKit disabled). Provides compatibility with older Docker versions and specific build scenarios that require the original builder implementation.

Full Description

The docker build --disable-buildkit command constructs Docker images using the original Docker builder engine, bypassing BuildKit optimizations. For your anihpj Django project, this legacy builder provides compatibility when working with older Docker versions, specific enterprise environments, or build scenarios that require the traditional layer-based building approach without advanced BuildKit features.

When building your anihpj Django application with legacy builder, the process reads your Dockerfile sequentially, creates intermediate containers for each instruction, and builds layers using the traditional Docker daemon. This approach is useful for debugging build issues, maintaining compatibility with legacy CI/CD pipelines, or working in environments where BuildKit features are not available or desired.

Syntax

docker build --disable-buildkit [OPTIONS] PATH | URL | -
Parameters
  • --disable-buildkit - Disable BuildKit and use legacy builder
  • --build-arg - Set build-time variables
  • --cache-from - Images to consider as cache sources
  • --file, -f - Name of the Dockerfile
  • --force-rm - Always remove intermediate containers
  • --isolation - Container isolation technology
  • --label - Set metadata for an image
  • --network - Set the networking mode for RUN instructions
  • --no-cache - Do not use cache when building the image
  • --platform - Set platform if server is multi-platform capable
  • --pull - Always attempt to pull a newer version of the image
  • --quiet, -q - Suppress the build output
  • --rm - Remove intermediate containers after successful build (default true)
  • --tag, -t - Name and optionally a tag in 'name:tag' format
  • --target - Set the target build stage
  • --ulimit - Ulimit options
Attributes
  • Builder Engine: Legacy Docker builder (without BuildKit)
  • Cache Strategy: Layer-based caching only
  • Multi-platform: Limited support
  • Build Context: Full context transfer
  • Compatibility: Docker Engine 1.8+
Methods
  • build() - Execute the legacy build process
  • cache() - Manage layer-based cache
  • tag() - Apply tags to built image
  • export() - Save image to tar archive
  • cleanup() - Remove build artifacts
Return Values
  • Success: Image ID and build log
  • Error: Build failure details and exit code
  • Warnings: Deprecated feature notices
  • Cache Stats: Layer reuse information
Examples
Example 1: Basic Legacy Build
# Build Django app using legacy builder
docker build --disable-buildkit -t anihpj:legacy .

# Create legacy-specific Dockerfile
cat > Dockerfile.legacy << 'EOF'
FROM python:3.11-slim
WORKDIR /app
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install -r requirements.txt
COPY . .
EXPOSE 8000
CMD ["python", "manage.py", "runserver", "0.0.0.0:8000"]
EOF

# Build with legacy builder
docker build --disable-buildkit -f Dockerfile.legacy -t anihpj:legacy-v1.0 .
Image built successfully using legacy builder without BuildKit optimizations
Example 2: Legacy Build with Cache Control
# Build with no cache for clean legacy build
docker build --disable-buildkit --no-cache -t anihpj:fresh .

# Use specific cache source for legacy builds
docker build --disable-buildkit --cache-from anihpj:base -t anihpj:cached .

# Build with build arguments for legacy compatibility
docker build --disable-buildkit \
  --build-arg DJANGO_VERSION=4.2 \
  --build-arg PYTHON_VERSION=3.11 \
  -t anihpj:legacy-args .
Legacy build with controlled cache behavior and build-time variables
Example 3: Multi-stage Legacy Build
# Multi-stage Dockerfile for legacy builds
cat > Dockerfile.multistage << 'EOF'
# Build stage
FROM python:3.11 AS builder
WORKDIR /build
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --user -r requirements.txt

# Production stage
FROM python:3.11-slim
COPY --from=builder /root/.local /root/.local
WORKDIR /app
COPY . .
ENV PATH=/root/.local/bin:$PATH
EXPOSE 8000
CMD ["python", "manage.py", "runserver", "0.0.0.0:8000"]
EOF

# Build specific stage with legacy builder
docker build --disable-buildkit --target builder -t anihpj:builder-legacy .
docker build --disable-buildkit -f Dockerfile.multistage -t anihpj:production-legacy .
Multi-stage build using legacy builder with reduced final image size
Example 4: Legacy Build with Network Control
# Build with host network for legacy compatibility
docker build --disable-buildkit --network host -t anihpj:network .

# Build with custom network for legacy builds
docker network create anihpj-build
docker build --disable-buildkit --network anihpj-build -t anihpj:custom-net .

# Build with isolated network (default)
docker build --disable-buildkit --network none -t anihpj:isolated .
Legacy builds with different network configurations for compatibility
Example 5: Legacy Build with Labels and Metadata
# Build with comprehensive labels using legacy builder
docker build --disable-buildkit \
  --label "app.name=anihpj" \
  --label "app.version=1.0.0" \
  --label "app.description=Django Job Portal" \
  --label "app.maintainer=dev@anihpj.com" \
  --label "build.type=legacy" \
  --label "build.date=$(date -u +'%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ')" \
  -t anihpj:labeled .

# Build with Git metadata for legacy tracking
docker build --disable-buildkit \
  --label "vcs.ref=$(git rev-parse HEAD)" \
  --label "vcs.url=https://github.com/user/anihpj" \
  --build-arg GIT_COMMIT=$(git rev-parse HEAD) \
  -t anihpj:git-legacy .
Legacy build with comprehensive metadata and version tracking

docker builder prune

Official Docs: docker builder prune | Docker CLI: docker builder prune

Remove build cache and unused builder resources. Cleans up build artifacts, intermediate layers, and cache entries to free up disk space and maintain a clean build environment.

Full Description

The docker builder prune command removes BuildKit build cache and unused builder resources to reclaim disk space. For your anihpj Django project development workflow, this command helps maintain clean build environments by removing cached layers, intermediate build artifacts, and unused cache entries that accumulate during frequent Django application builds and testing cycles.

Regular cache pruning is essential when developing your anihpj Django application with multiple build iterations, dependency updates, or when switching between development branches. The command intelligently preserves active cache while removing stale entries, optimizing storage usage without significantly impacting build performance for your jobpost app and related Django components.

Syntax

docker builder prune [OPTIONS]
Parameters
  • --all, -a - Remove all unused build cache, not just dangling ones
  • --filter - Provide filter values (e.g., 'until=24h')
  • --force, -f - Do not prompt for confirmation
  • --keep-storage - Amount of disk space to keep for cache
Attributes
  • Cache Types: Build cache, layer cache, source cache
  • Storage Impact: Disk space recovery
  • Scope: All builders or specific builder
  • Safety: Preserves active and recent cache
  • Performance: May slow down subsequent builds
Methods
  • prune() - Execute cache cleanup
  • analyze() - Estimate space to be freed
  • filter() - Apply selective pruning
  • preserve() - Maintain essential cache
  • report() - Show cleanup results
Return Values
  • Success: Amount of space reclaimed
  • Cache IDs: List of removed cache entries
  • Preserved: Cache entries kept for performance
  • Statistics: Before/after disk usage
Examples
Example 1: Basic Builder Cache Cleanup
# Clean up build cache interactively
docker builder prune

# Force cleanup without confirmation
docker builder prune --force

# Check current cache usage before cleanup
docker system df
docker builder prune -f
docker system df
Cleaned up build cache, reclaimed 2.5GB of disk space
Example 2: Aggressive Cache Cleanup
# Remove all build cache, including active cache
docker builder prune --all --force

# Clean up cache older than 24 hours
docker builder prune --filter "until=24h" --force

# Clean up cache older than 7 days
docker builder prune --filter "until=168h" --force
Aggressive cleanup removed all unused build cache, including recent entries
Example 3: Selective Cache Cleanup with Storage Limits
# Keep 10GB of cache storage
docker builder prune --keep-storage 10GB --force

# Keep 50% of current cache
CURRENT_SIZE=$(docker system df -v | grep 'Build Cache' | awk '{print $2}')
docker builder prune --keep-storage $((CURRENT_SIZE / 2))

# Keep minimal cache (1GB)
docker builder prune --keep-storage 1GB --all --force
Maintained 10GB cache while cleaning 15GB of older build artifacts
Example 4: Scheduled Cache Maintenance
# Create maintenance script for anihpj project
cat > docker-cleanup.sh << 'EOF'
#!/bin/bash
echo "Starting Docker build cache cleanup..."
echo "Current cache usage:"
docker system df

echo "Pruning build cache older than 48 hours..."
docker builder prune --filter "until=48h" --force

echo "After cleanup:"
docker system df
EOF

chmod +x docker-cleanup.sh

# Run weekly cleanup
./docker-cleanup.sh
Automated cleanup script removed 3.2GB of build cache older than 48 hours
Example 5: Development Workflow Cache Management
# Development cycle with cache management
echo "Building anihpj Django app..."
docker build -t anihpj:dev .

echo "Current build cache:"
docker system df --format "table {{.Type}}\t{{.TotalCount}}\t{{.Size}}\t{{.Reclaimable}}"

# Clean old cache before major rebuild
echo "Cleaning cache older than 12 hours before production build..."
docker builder prune --filter "until=12h" -f

# Production build with fresh dependencies
echo "Building production image..."
docker build --no-cache -t anihpj:production .

# Post-build cleanup
echo "Final cleanup of development artifacts..."
docker builder prune --filter "label=stage=development" -f
Development workflow maintained efficient cache usage: 8.5GB active, 4.2GB cleaned

docker buildx dap

Official Docs: docker buildx dap | Docker CLI: docker buildx dap

Debug Access Point (DAP) for BuildKit debugging. Provides advanced debugging capabilities for build processes, allowing developers to inspect, attach to, and troubleshoot complex build scenarios.

Syntax

docker buildx dap [COMMAND] [OPTIONS]

Parameters Description

  • --builder: Override configured builder instance for targeted debugging sessions
  • --debug: Enable debug output for comprehensive build process visibility
  • --verbose: Show verbose output for detailed DAP operation logs
Parameters
  • --builder - Override the configured builder instance
  • --debug - Enable debug output
  • --verbose - Show verbose output
Attributes
  • Protocol: Debug Access Protocol
  • Scope: BuildKit debugging and inspection
  • Access Level: Deep build process visibility
  • Compatibility: BuildKit 0.10+
  • Use Case: Advanced debugging scenarios
Methods
  • attach() - Connect to running build process
  • inspect() - Examine build state
  • trace() - Follow execution flow
  • breakpoint() - Set debugging breakpoints
  • analyze() - Performance analysis
Return Values
  • Debug Session: Active debugging connection
  • Build State: Current process information
  • Performance Data: Timing and resource usage
  • Error Details: Detailed failure analysis
Examples
Example 1: DAP Session Initialization

docker buildx dap attach

Official Docs: docker buildx dap attach | Docker CLI: docker buildx dap attach

Attach to an active or completed build process for debugging. Provides real-time access to build execution for monitoring and troubleshooting.

Syntax

docker buildx dap attach [OPTIONS] [BUILD_ID]
Parameters
  • BUILD_ID - Specific build to attach to
  • --builder - Builder instance to attach to
  • --wait - Wait for new builds to attach
  • --follow - Follow build progress
  • --interactive - Enable interactive debugging
Attributes
  • Attachment Type: Real-time or post-mortem
  • Session State: Active monitoring connection
  • Debugging Level: Full build process access
  • Performance Impact: Minimal overhead
  • Compatibility: All BuildKit versions
Methods
  • connect() - Establish debug connection
  • monitor() - Watch build progress
  • step() - Step through execution
  • break() - Pause execution
  • detach() - Disconnect from session
Return Values
  • Session ID: Unique debugging session identifier
  • Build Status: Current execution state
  • Debug Interface: Interactive debugging capabilities
  • Log Stream: Real-time build output
Examples
Example 1: Attach to Running Build
# Start a Django build in background
docker buildx build -t anihpj:debug . &

# Find the build ID
BUILD_ID=$(docker buildx ls --builder mybuilder | grep "running" | awk '{print $1}')

# Attach to the running build
docker buildx dap attach $BUILD_ID --follow
Attached to build abc123, monitoring Django dependency installation
Example 2: Interactive Debugging Session
# Attach with interactive debugging
docker buildx dap attach --interactive --builder mybuilder

# Wait for next build and auto-attach
docker buildx dap attach --wait --follow

# Attach to specific failed build
docker buildx dap attach build-failed-456 --interactive
Interactive debug session started, breakpoints available
Example 3: Multi-stage Build Attachment
# Attach to specific build stage
docker buildx dap attach --stage dependencies

# Monitor stage progression
docker buildx dap attach --follow --stage-verbose

# Attach to final stage only
docker buildx dap attach --stage production --wait
Attached to dependencies stage, pip install progress visible
Example 4: Builder-specific Attachment
# Attach to specific builder instance
docker buildx dap attach --builder anihpj-builder --follow

# Monitor all builds on builder
docker buildx dap attach --builder mybuilder --wait --all

# Attach with builder context
docker buildx dap attach --builder container --interactive
Monitoring all builds on anihpj-builder, 2 active sessions
Example 5: Automated Debugging Workflow
# Create debug monitoring script
cat > debug-monitor.sh << 'EOF'
#!/bin/bash
echo "Starting automated debug monitoring..."

# Attach to next build with logging
docker buildx dap attach --wait --follow \
  > build-debug.log 2>&1 &

# Monitor for errors
tail -f build-debug.log | grep -E "(ERROR|FAILED)" &

echo "Debug monitoring active, logs in build-debug.log"
EOF

chmod +x debug-monitor.sh
./debug-monitor.sh
Automated debugging active, will attach to next anihpj build

#!/bin/bash
echo "Starting comprehensive debug build for anihpj..."

# Stage 1: Interactive debug build
docker buildx debug build \
  --debug-interactive \
  --debug-level trace \
  --profile \
  --trace-file anihpj-debug-$(date +%H%M).json \
  --breakpoint-on-error \
  -t anihpj:debug-$(date +%Y%m%d) .

# Stage 2: Analyze results
echo "Analyzing debug results..."
docker buildx debug analyze-trace anihpj-debug-$(date +%H%M).json

# Stage 3: Generate report
docker buildx debug report --trace anihpj-debug-$(date +%H%M).json --output debug-report.html
EOF

chmod +x debug-build.sh
./debug-build.sh
Comprehensive debug workflow: trace saved, report generated at debug-report.html

docker buildx du

Official Docs: docker buildx du | Docker CLI: docker buildx du

Display disk usage of BuildKit build cache. Shows detailed information about cache consumption, build artifacts, and storage utilization across different builders and platforms.

Full Description

The docker buildx du command provides detailed analysis of BuildKit cache disk usage across all configured builders. For your anihpj Django project, this command is essential for monitoring storage consumption during development cycles, identifying cache bloat from frequent builds, and making informed decisions about cache cleanup to maintain optimal development environment performance.

When managing your anihpj Django application builds, use this command to track cache growth patterns, identify which components consume the most storage, analyze cache efficiency across different builders, and plan storage management strategies. The detailed breakdown helps optimize your development workflow by understanding cache utilization for your jobpost app, user management, and related Django components.

Syntax

# Check current cache usage
docker buildx du

# Detailed analysis with verbose output
docker buildx du --verbose

# JSON format for automation
docker buildx du --format json

# Specific builder analysis
docker buildx du --builder anihpj-builder

# Filter by cache age
docker buildx du --filter "until=24h"

Parameters Description

  • --builder: Specify builder instance for targeted analysis. Essential for multi-builder setups in your anihpj Django project where different builders may have varying cache patterns and storage requirements.
  • --filter: Apply filters to cache entries (until, size, label). Use to analyze specific cache subsets, such as entries older than a certain time or larger than specified sizes, helping optimize your Django build cache strategy.
  • --format: Output format control (table, json, yaml). JSON format enables integration with monitoring scripts and automated cache management tools for your anihpj project infrastructure.
  • --verbose: Show detailed cache breakdown including individual layer information. Provides granular insights into cache composition for your Django application's build layers and dependencies.

Attributes

  • Storage Metrics: Total cache size, active cache, and reclaimable space
  • Cache Analysis: Breakdown by builder, age, and usage patterns
  • Layer Details: Individual cache entry sizes and timestamps
  • Builder Scope: Per-builder cache utilization and comparison
  • Time Tracking: Cache age analysis for cleanup planning

Methods

  • analyze_usage() - Calculate and categorize disk consumption
  • breakdown_cache() - Organize cache by type, age, and builder
  • calculate_efficiency() - Determine cache hit rates and utilization
  • identify_bloat() - Find oversized or stale cache entries
  • generate_report() - Create comprehensive usage analysis

Return Values

  • Total Usage: Overall cache disk consumption across all builders
  • Detailed Breakdown: Per-builder and per-entry cache statistics
  • Efficiency Metrics: Cache utilization rates and effectiveness
  • Cleanup Potential: Estimated space reclaimable through pruning
  • Trend Analysis: Historical cache growth and usage patterns

Syntax

docker buildx du [OPTIONS]
Parameters
  • --builder - Specify builder instance
  • --filter - Filter cache entries
  • --format - Output format (table, json, yaml)
  • --verbose - Show detailed information
Attributes
  • Storage Analysis: Comprehensive disk usage
  • Cache Breakdown: Per-layer and per-build data
  • Builder Scope: Multiple builder support
  • Platform Details: Cross-platform cache usage
  • Time Tracking: Cache age and last access
Methods
  • analyze_usage() - Calculate disk consumption
  • categorize_cache() - Organize by type/age
  • estimate_savings() - Potential cleanup savings
  • track_trends() - Usage over time
  • export_report() - Generate usage reports
Return Values
  • Total Usage: Overall cache disk consumption
  • Cache Details: Individual cache entry sizes
  • Cleanup Potential: Reclaimable space estimates
  • Usage Trends: Historical consumption patterns
Examples
Example 1: Basic Disk Usage Analysis
# Check BuildKit cache usage
docker buildx du

# Detailed cache analysis
docker buildx du --verbose

# JSON format output
docker buildx du --format json | jq .
Total cache usage: 4.2GB (15 cache entries, oldest: 7 days ago)
Example 2: Builder-specific Usage
# Check specific builder usage
docker buildx du --builder anihpj-builder

# Compare builders
docker buildx du --builder mybuilder --verbose
docker buildx du --builder container --verbose

# All builders summary
for builder in $(docker buildx ls --format '{{.Name}}'); do
  echo "Builder: $builder"
  docker buildx du --builder $builder
  echo
done
anihpj-builder: 2.1GB, mybuilder: 1.8GB, container: 0.3GB
Example 3: Filtered Cache Analysis
# Filter by cache age
docker buildx du --filter "until=24h"

# Filter by size
docker buildx du --filter "size>100MB"

# Filter by build context
docker buildx du --filter "context=anihpj" --verbose
Filtered results: 8 cache entries matching criteria, 1.4GB total
Example 4: Cache Usage Monitoring
# Create usage monitoring script
cat > cache-monitor.sh << 'EOF'
#!/bin/bash
DATESTAMP=$(date +%Y%m%d_%H%M)
echo "[$DATESTAMP] Docker BuildX Cache Usage Report" > cache-report-$DATESTAMP.txt

docker buildx du --format json > cache-data-$DATESTAMP.json
docker buildx du --verbose >> cache-report-$DATESTAMP.txt

echo "Report saved: cache-report-$DATESTAMP.txt"
EOF

chmod +x cache-monitor.sh
./cache-monitor.sh
Cache monitoring report generated: cache-report-20260111_1430.txt
Example 5: Django Project Cache Analysis
# Analyze anihpj project cache usage
echo "Analyzing cache usage for anihpj Django project..."

# Overall usage
docker buildx du --verbose

# Calculate cache efficiency
TOTAL_SIZE=$(docker buildx du --format json | jq -r '.Total.Size')
ACTIVE_SIZE=$(docker buildx du --format json | jq -r '.Active.Size')
EFFICIENCY=$((ACTIVE_SIZE * 100 / TOTAL_SIZE))

echo "Cache efficiency: ${EFFICIENCY}% (${ACTIVE_SIZE}/${TOTAL_SIZE})"

# Recommend cleanup if efficiency is low
if [ $EFFICIENCY -lt 50 ]; then
  echo "Recommendation: Run 'docker buildx prune' to improve efficiency"
fi

# Export detailed analysis
docker buildx du --format json > anihpj-cache-analysis.json
anihpj cache analysis: 68% efficiency, 2.8GB active of 4.1GB total

docker buildx history

Official Docs: docker buildx history | Docker CLI: docker buildx history

Manage and analyze BuildKit build history. Provides comprehensive tracking of build executions and performance metrics for optimization.

Full Description

The docker buildx history command provides comprehensive tracking and analysis of BuildKit build executions. For your anihpj Django project, this command enables performance monitoring, build trend analysis, troubleshooting failed builds, and optimization of your development workflow by maintaining detailed records of all build activities including timing, success rates, and resource utilization.

When developing your anihpj Django application, build history becomes invaluable for identifying performance bottlenecks, tracking improvements over time, debugging recurring build issues, and maintaining quality metrics for your jobpost app deployments. The historical data helps optimize cache strategies, identify dependency update impacts, and ensure consistent build performance across development cycles.

Syntax

# Show recent build history
docker buildx history

# Detailed history analysis
docker buildx history --verbose --limit 20

# Export history data
docker buildx history --format json > anihpj-builds.json

# Filter by date range
docker buildx history --filter "since=7d"

# Analyze failed builds
docker buildx history --filter "status=failed"

Parameters Description

  • --builder: Target specific builder instance for history analysis. Essential when managing multiple builders for your anihpj Django project with different purposes (development, staging, production).
  • --format: Control output format (table, json, yaml). JSON format enables automated analysis and integration with monitoring systems tracking your Django application build performance.
  • --limit: Restrict number of history entries returned. Useful for performance when analyzing recent builds or when integrating with scripts monitoring your anihpj project build trends.
  • --filter: Apply selection criteria (since, status, builder). Filter by build age, success/failure status, or specific builders to focus analysis on relevant build subset for your Django workflow.

Attributes

  • Build Records: Complete execution history with timestamps and metadata
  • Performance Metrics: Build duration, cache utilization, and resource usage
  • Success Tracking: Build outcomes and failure analysis capabilities
  • Trend Analysis: Historical patterns and performance evolution
  • Export Support: Data portability for external analysis tools

Methods

  • track_builds() - Monitor and record build execution details
  • analyze_performance() - Calculate metrics and identify trends
  • filter_records() - Apply selection criteria to build history
  • export_data() - Generate reports in various formats
  • compare_builds() - Analyze performance changes over time

Return Values

  • Build Timeline: Chronological record of all build executions
  • Performance Data: Duration, resource usage, and efficiency metrics
  • Success Metrics: Build outcome statistics and reliability data
  • Trend Reports: Historical analysis and pattern identification
  • Diagnostic Info: Failure analysis and troubleshooting data

Syntax

docker buildx history [COMMAND] [OPTIONS]
Parameters
  • --builder - Specify builder instance
  • --format - Output format (table, json, yaml)
  • --limit - Limit number of history entries
  • --filter - Filter history entries
Attributes
  • History Tracking: Complete build execution records
  • Performance Metrics: Build timing and resource usage
  • Trend Analysis: Build performance over time
  • Error Tracking: Historical failure analysis
  • Export Capabilities: Data export and analysis
Methods
  • list_builds() - Show build history
  • analyze_trends() - Performance trend analysis
  • export_data() - Export history data
  • import_data() - Import history records
  • trace_execution() - Detailed build traces
Return Values
  • Build Records: Historical build information
  • Performance Data: Timing and resource metrics
  • Trend Reports: Analysis over time periods
  • Export Files: Data in various formats
Examples
Example 1: View Build History
# Show recent build history
docker buildx history

# Detailed history with metadata
docker buildx history --verbose

# JSON format for analysis
docker buildx history --format json
Build history: 23 builds over 14 days, average duration 2m 34s
Example 2: Filtered History Analysis
# Filter by date range
docker buildx history --filter "since=2024-01-01"

# Filter by build status
docker buildx history --filter "status=failed"

# Filter by builder
docker buildx history --filter "builder=anihpj-builder" --limit 10
Filtered history: 5 failed builds found, investigating common issues
Example 3: Performance Trend Analysis
# Analyze build performance trends
docker buildx history --format json | jq '.[] | {date: .created, duration: .duration}'

# Calculate average build times
docker buildx history --format json | jq '[.[] | .duration] | add / length'

# Find slowest builds
docker buildx history --format json | jq 'sort_by(.duration) | reverse | .[0:5]'
Performance trends: 15% improvement over last month, cache hit rate: 82%
Example 4: Django Project History Tracking
# Track anihpj project build history
echo "Analyzing anihpj Django project build history..."

# Recent builds for this project
docker buildx history --filter "tag=anihpj*" --limit 20

# Export project-specific history
docker buildx history --filter "context=." --format json > anihpj-build-history.json

# Generate build report
cat > build-report.sh << 'EOF'
#!/bin/bash
echo "AnihPJ Build History Report - $(date)"
echo "======================================"

TOTAL_BUILDS=$(docker buildx history --format json | jq 'length')
SUCCESS_BUILDS=$(docker buildx history --filter "status=success" --format json | jq 'length')
FAILED_BUILDS=$(docker buildx history --filter "status=failed" --format json | jq 'length')

echo "Total builds: $TOTAL_BUILDS"
echo "Successful: $SUCCESS_BUILDS"
echo "Failed: $FAILED_BUILDS"
echo "Success rate: $(($SUCCESS_BUILDS * 100 / $TOTAL_BUILDS))%"
EOF

chmod +x build-report.sh
./build-report.sh
AnihPJ build report: 18 total builds, 16 successful, 2 failed (89% success rate)
Example 5: Advanced History Management
# Comprehensive history management workflow
echo "Starting comprehensive build history analysis..."

# Export complete history
docker buildx history --format json > complete-history.json

# Analyze build patterns
echo "Build frequency analysis:"
docker buildx history --format json | jq 'group_by(.created[:10]) | map({date: .[0].created[:10], count: length}) | sort_by(.date)'

# Performance analysis
echo "Performance metrics:"
docker buildx history --format json | jq '{
  total_builds: length,
  avg_duration: ([.[] | .duration] | add / length),
  max_duration: ([.[] | .duration] | max),
  min_duration: ([.[] | .duration] | min)
}'

# Cache effectiveness
echo "Cache analysis:"
docker buildx history --format json | jq '[.[] | select(.cache_hit)] | length' cache_hits
echo "Cache hit rate: $(echo "scale=2; $cache_hits * 100 / $TOTAL_BUILDS" | bc)%"

# Generate comprehensive report
docker buildx history export --format detailed > anihpj-detailed-history.txt
Advanced analysis complete: detailed patterns identified, optimization recommendations generated

docker buildx imagetools create

Official Docs: docker buildx imagetools create | Docker CLI: docker buildx imagetools create

Create multi-platform manifest and push to registry. Combines platform-specific images into unified multi-architecture manifests.

Full Description

The docker buildx imagetools create command creates multi-platform manifests by combining platform-specific images into unified registry entries. For your anihpj Django project, this command enables deployment to diverse architectures (x86_64, ARM64) from a single manifest, allowing your jobpost application to run efficiently across cloud providers, edge devices, and various computing environments.

When deploying your anihpj Django application across multiple platforms, this command consolidates individually built images (linux/amd64, linux/arm64) into a single manifest that automatically selects the appropriate architecture. This approach optimizes deployment flexibility, reduces registry storage, and simplifies container orchestration for your Django app across heterogeneous infrastructure.

Syntax

# Create multi-platform manifest
docker buildx imagetools create -t anihpj:latest anihpj:amd64 anihpj:arm64

# Append to existing manifest
docker buildx imagetools create --append -t anihpj:v2.0 anihpj:new-arch

# Dry run to preview changes
docker buildx imagetools create --dry-run -t anihpj:test anihpj:amd64 anihpj:arm64

Parameters Description

  • --tag, -t: Target manifest name and tag. Use descriptive tags like anihpj:v1.0 or anihpj:production to clearly identify different versions of your Django application's multi-platform manifest.
  • --append: Add to existing manifest instead of replacing. Useful for incrementally adding platform support to your anihpj Django app without recreating the entire manifest structure.
  • --dry-run: Preview changes without execution. Verify manifest composition before pushing to production registries, essential for validating your Django app's multi-platform deployment strategy.
  • --progress: Control progress output during manifest creation. Use 'plain' for CI/CD pipelines deploying your anihpj Django application to get detailed operation logs.

Attributes

  • Platform Unification: Combines multiple architecture images into single manifest
  • Registry Integration: Seamless push to container registries with manifest support
  • Architecture Detection: Automatic platform selection based on deployment target
  • Incremental Updates: Append functionality for adding new platforms
  • Validation Support: Dry-run capability for manifest verification

Methods

  • combine_platforms() - Merge platform-specific images into unified manifest
  • validate_images() - Verify source image compatibility and availability
  • create_manifest() - Generate multi-platform manifest structure
  • push_registry() - Upload manifest to container registry
  • verify_result() - Confirm successful manifest creation and accessibility

Return Values

  • Manifest Creation: Successful multi-platform manifest with platform list
  • Registry Location: Complete manifest reference and accessibility details
  • Platform Summary: List of included architectures and their image details
  • Operation Status: Creation success confirmation and any warnings
  • Verification Data: Manifest integrity and registry synchronization status

Examples

Example: Create Multi-platform Manifest
# Create multi-arch manifest for anihpj
docker buildx imagetools create \
  --tag anihpj:multiarch \
  anihpj:amd64 \
  anihpj:arm64

# Append to existing manifest
docker buildx imagetools create \
  --append \
  --tag anihpj:latest \
  anihpj:new-platform
Multi-platform manifest created: anihpj:multiarch (amd64, arm64)

List all available builder instances. Shows builder names, drivers, status, and supported platforms for your Docker build environment.

Full Description

The docker buildx ls command displays all configured builder instances in your Docker environment. For your anihpj Django project, this command helps you identify available builders, their current status, supported platforms (like linux/amd64, linux/arm64), and driver types (docker, docker-container, kubernetes). This is essential for managing multi-platform builds and understanding your build infrastructure capabilities.

When developing your anihpj Django application, use this command to verify builder configurations before executing complex builds, ensure cross-platform support is available, and troubleshoot build environment issues.   • Name Display: Shows builder instance names with active indicator (*)
  • Driver Information: Lists driver type (docker, docker-container, kubernetes)

  • list_builders() - Enumerate all available builder instances

  • Status Information: Current operational state of each builder
  • Platform Support: Available architectures for each builder
  • Driver Details: Builder implementation and capabilities
  • Active Indicator: Currently selected builder marked with asterisk

docker buildx ls --format json

# Filter active builders
docker buildx ls --filter "status=running"
NAME DRIVER STATUS PLATFORMS anihpj-builder * docker-container running linux/amd64, linux/arm64 default docker running linux/amd64

docker buildx prune

Official Docs: docker buildx prune | Docker CLI: docker buildx prune

Remove build cache and unused builder resources. Cleans up BuildKit cache to free disk space and maintain optimal build performance for your Django development workflow.

Full Description

The docker buildx prune command removes BuildKit build cache and unused builder resources to reclaim disk space. For your anihpj Django project, this command is essential for maintaining clean development environments by removing cached layers from previous builds, outdated dependencies, and intermediate build artifacts that accumulate during iterative Django development cycles.

Regular cache pruning optimizes your anihpj Django development workflow by preventing disk space exhaustion, improving build performance, and ensuring clean builds when needed. The command intelligently preserves recent and frequently-used cache while removing stale entries, balancing storage efficiency with build speed for your jobpost app and related Django components.

Syntax

# Remove unused cache
docker buildx prune

# Remove all cache
docker buildx prune --all --force

# Keep specific amount of cache
docker buildx prune --keep-storage 5GB --force

Parameters Description

  • --all, -a: Remove all cache, not just dangling entries. Use when you need complete cache cleanup for your anihpj Django project, such as after major dependency updates or when troubleshooting build issues.
  • --filter: Filter cache entries by criteria such as age, size, or labels. Useful for targeted cleanup of specific build cache related to particular anihpj Django app components or development branches.
  • --force, -f: Don't prompt for confirmation. Essential for automated CI/CD pipelines managing your Django project builds and deployments.
  • --keep-storage: Keep specified amount of cache storage. Maintains performance balance by preserving recent cache for your Django builds while cleaning older entries.

Attributes

  • Cache Intelligence: Distinguishes between active and stale cache
  • Storage Optimization: Reclaims disk space efficiently
  • Performance Balance: Maintains useful cache while cleaning unused
  • Selective Removal: Supports filtering and conditional cleanup
  • Automation Support: Force mode for unattended operations

Methods

  • analyze_cache() - Identify unused and stale cache entries
  • selective_prune() - Remove cache based on criteria
  • preserve_active() - Maintain frequently-used cache
  • estimate_savings() - Calculate potential space reclamation
  • execute_cleanup() - Perform cache removal operations

Return Values

  • Space Reclaimed: Amount of disk space freed by pruning
  • Cache Statistics: Before and after cache usage summary
  • Cleanup Report: Details of removed and preserved cache
  • Performance Impact: Expected effect on subsequent builds
  • Storage Status: Current cache utilization after cleanup

Examples

Example: Prune Build Cache
# Remove unused cache
docker buildx prune

# Remove all cache
docker buildx prune --all --force

# Keep 5GB of cache
docker buildx prune --keep-storage 5GB --force
Pruned build cache: 3.2GB reclaimed, 1.8GB remaining

  • --all-inactive: Remove all inactive builders to clean up unused build resources
  • --force: Force removal without confirmation, essential for automated cleanup scripts
  • --keep-daemon: Preserve daemon while removing builder configuration
  • --keep-state: Maintain builder state files for potential restoration
Parameters

  • --keep-daemon - Keep daemon running
  • --keep-state - Keep builder state

docker buildx use

Official Docs: docker buildx use | Docker CLI: docker buildx use

Set the default builder instance. Changes the active builder for subsequent build operations, essential for managing build environments and multi-platform deployments.

Full Description

The docker buildx use command sets the default builder instance for subsequent build operations. For your anihpj Django project, this command allows you to switch between different build environments, such as local development builders, CI/CD builders, or multi-platform builders optimized for cross-architecture deployments of your jobpost application.

When developing your anihpj Django application across different environments, use this command to switch to builders with specific capabilities - development builders for rapid iteration, production builders with multi-platform support for deployment, or specialized builders with custom configurations for your Django app's unique requirements including ARM64 support for cost-effective cloud deployments.

Syntax

# Use specific builder
docker buildx use anihpj-builder

# Set as default globally  
docker buildx use --default --global anihpj-builder

# Switch back to default
docker buildx use default

Parameters Description

  • BUILDER: Builder instance name to activate. Specify builders configured for your anihpj Django project needs, such as development, staging, or production environments with different platform support.
  • --default: Set as default builder for the current Docker context. Makes the builder persistent across Docker CLI sessions for consistent build behavior in your Django development workflow.
  • --global: Set globally for all Docker contexts. Ensures the builder selection persists across different Docker contexts and environments in your anihpj project infrastructure.

Attributes

  • Context Scope: Builder selection applies to current or global context
  • Session Persistence: Selection persists across Docker CLI sessions
  • Environment Isolation: Different builders for different development stages
  • Platform Switching: Access to different architecture support
  • Configuration Inheritance: Adopts builder's capabilities and settings

Methods

  • switch_builder() - Change active builder instance
  • set_default() - Configure persistent builder selection
  • apply_global() - Set builder across all contexts
  • validate_builder() - Verify builder availability and status
  • inherit_config() - Adopt builder capabilities and settings

Return Values

  • Selection Confirmation: Verification of active builder change
  • Builder Information: Details about newly active builder
  • Context Status: Scope of builder selection (local/global)
  • Capability Summary: Available features and platform support
  • Configuration State: Persistence and inheritance details

Examples

Example: Switch Builder
# Use specific builder
docker buildx use anihpj-builder

# Set as default globally
docker buildx use --default --global anihpj-builder

# Switch back to default
docker buildx use default
Switched to builder: anihpj-builder (docker-container)

📸 Checkpoint Commands

Docker checkpoint commands provide container state management capabilities for creating, managing, and restoring container checkpoints. For your anihpj Django project, these commands enable you to save running container states during development, testing, or debugging phases, allowing you to restart containers from specific execution points with preserved application state, database connections, and in-memory data. Note that containers are restarted from checkpoints using standard docker start commands with the checkpoint reference.

📋 Checkpoint Commands Overview

docker checkpoint - Container checkpoint management and operations
docker checkpoint create - Create container checkpoints
docker checkpoint ls - List container checkpoints
docker checkpoint rm - Remove container checkpoints
🎯 Django Focus: This documentation shows practical examples of managing container checkpoints for Django development workflows using the anihpj project. Learn how to preserve container states during debugging, create development snapshots, and manage checkpoint storage for Django applications with jobpost app models (Skills, User, Author, Location, JobPost).

docker checkpoint

Official Docs: docker checkpoint | Docker CLI: docker checkpoint

Container state management and checkpoint operations for running containers.

Full Description

The docker checkpoint command provides container state management capabilities for creating, listing, and removing container checkpoints. For your anihpj Django project, this command group enables you to save and restore container states during development, debugging, and testing workflows, preserving application memory, process states, and runtime configurations.

Syntax

# View available checkpoint subcommands
docker checkpoint --help

# Common checkpoint operations for Django containers
docker checkpoint create anihpj_web debug_snapshot
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_web
docker checkpoint rm anihpj_web old_snapshot

Available Subcommands for anihpj Django Development

  • create: Create new checkpoints of running Django containers, preserving application state and memory contents.
  • ls: List all existing checkpoints for Django containers, showing creation timestamps and checkpoint names.
  • rm: Remove unused checkpoints to free disk space and maintain clean checkpoint history.

Django-Specific Use Cases

Development Workflow Integration
# Checkpoint before major refactoring
docker checkpoint create --leave anihpj_web pre_refactor_state

# List all development checkpoints
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_web

# Clean up completed debugging checkpoints
docker checkpoint rm anihpj_web debug_session_1 debug_session_2

Integrate checkpoint management into your Django development workflow for safer experimentation and debugging.

Real-world anihpj Examples

Complete Checkpoint Workflow
# Create checkpoint before testing JobPost feature
docker checkpoint create --leave anihpj_web jobpost_feature_test

# Verify checkpoint was created
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_web

# After testing, remove temporary checkpoint
docker checkpoint rm anihpj_web jobpost_feature_test
checkpoint created: anihpj_web:jobpost_feature_test CHECKPOINT NAME CREATED jobpost_feature_test just now checkpoint removed: anihpj_web:jobpost_feature_test

docker checkpoint create

Official Docs: docker checkpoint create | Docker CLI: docker checkpoint create

Create a checkpoint of a running container, preserving its current state.

Full Description

The docker checkpoint create command creates a checkpoint of a running container, preserving its current state including memory contents, process states, and open file descriptors. For your anihpj Django project, this command enables you to capture snapshots of running Django development servers, database containers, or background task processors, allowing you to restore these exact states later for debugging, testing, or development purposes.

When creating checkpoints for your anihpj Django application, the command saves the complete runtime state including Python interpreter memory, Django application cache, active database connections, session data, and any in-progress background tasks. This enables precise restoration of development environments, debugging scenarios, and testing states for your jobpost app components including Skills models, User authentication states, Author profiles, Location data processing, and JobPost operations.

Syntax

# Basic checkpoint creation
docker checkpoint create {container} {checkpoint-name}

# Create checkpoint and leave container running
docker checkpoint create --leave {container} {checkpoint-name}

# Create checkpoint for Django web server
docker checkpoint create anihpj_web development_snapshot

# Create checkpoint with descriptive name
docker checkpoint create --leave anihpj_web debug_jobpost_feature

# Create database checkpoint during testing
docker checkpoint create anihpj_db test_data_snapshot

Parameters Description

  • container: Name or ID of the running Django container to checkpoint. Must be in running state to capture live application memory and process states.
  • checkpoint-name: Unique identifier for the checkpoint. Use descriptive names like 'debug_session', 'pre_migration', or 'feature_test_state' for Django development workflows.
  • --leave: Keep the container running after creating checkpoint. Essential for Django development when you want to continue working while preserving a restore point.

Attributes

  • Checkpoint ID: Unique identifier for the created checkpoint state. Used to reference specific checkpoint for restoration operations.
  • Creation Timestamp: Time when checkpoint was created. Helps track checkpoint history and identify most recent development states.
  • Container State Size: Disk space used by the checkpoint data. Monitor this for Django applications to manage storage efficiently.
  • Process Count: Number of processes captured in checkpoint. Includes Django server processes, database connections, and background tasks.
  • Memory Footprint: Amount of memory state preserved. Important for Django applications with large dataset caches or active user sessions.

Methods

Create with Continue Operation

Syntax: docker checkpoint create --leave {container} {checkpoint-name}

Parameters: container (string) - Running Django container name, checkpoint-name (string) - Checkpoint identifier

Create checkpoint while keeping Django development server running, enabling continuous development workflow with safety restore points.

Create for Debugging State

Syntax: docker checkpoint create {container} debug_{feature-name}

Parameters: container (string) - Django container with debug setup, feature-name (string) - Specific feature being debugged

Capture Django application state when encountering bugs, preserving exact conditions for later analysis and reproduction.

Return Value

Returns confirmation message with checkpoint name and container reference. On failure, returns error details about checkpoint creation issues.

  • Success (0): Checkpoint created successfully with confirmation message
  • Error (1): Container not running or checkpoint name already exists
  • Error (125): Invalid command usage or Docker daemon error

5 Practical Django Examples

Example 1: Development State Preservation (Skills Model Testing)
Example 1: Development State Preservation (Skills Model Testing)

Scenario: Creating checkpoint before testing new Skills model functionality to preserve stable development state.

# Create checkpoint of Django development environment

Django Models Context:

# Skills model being tested
class Skills(models.Model):
    name = models.CharField(max_length=100)
    category = models.CharField(max_length=50)
    level = models.CharField(max_length=20)
    created_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    
    def __str__(self):
        return f"{self.name} - {self.level}"

# Test data in development database
skills_data = [
    Skills(name="Python", category="Programming", level="Expert"),
    Skills(name="Django", category="Framework", level="Advanced"),
    Skills(name="PostgreSQL", category="Database", level="Intermediate")
]

# Print current skills count before testing
print(f"Current Skills count: {Skills.objects.count()}")
for skill in Skills.objects.all():
    print(f"Skill: {skill.name}, Category: {skill.category}, Level: {skill.level}")

class Location(models.Model):

Example 5: JobPost Processing State Checkpoint (Background Tasks)

Scenario: Creating checkpoint of Celery worker processing JobPost tasks to preserve queue state during heavy processing load.

# Create checkpoint of worker during JobPost processing
docker checkpoint create anihpj_worker jobpost_processing_state

Django Models Context:

# JobPost model with active processing
class JobPost(models.Model):
    title = models.CharField(max_length=200)
    description = models.TextField()
    author = models.ForeignKey(Author, on_delete=models.CASCADE)
    location = models.ForeignKey(Location, on_delete=models.CASCADE)
    required_skills = models.ManyToManyField(Skills)
    salary_min = models.DecimalField(max_digits=10, decimal_places=2, null=True, blank=True)
    salary_max = models.DecimalField(max_digits=10, decimal_places=2, null=True, blank=True)
    is_remote = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    is_published = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    created_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    updated_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now=True)
    
    def __str__(self):
        return self.title

# JobPost data being processed in background
jobposts_data = [
    JobPost(title="Senior Django Developer", description="Looking for experienced Django developer...", 
            author_id=1, location_id=1, salary_min=120000, salary_max=150000, is_remote=True, is_published=True),
    JobPost(title="Python Data Scientist", description="Data scientist role with Python focus...", 
            author_id=2, location_id=2, salary_min=100000, salary_max=130000, is_remote=False, is_published=True),
    JobPost(title="Full Stack Developer", description="Full stack position with Django and React...", 
            author_id=1, location_id=3, salary_min=90000, salary_max=120000, is_remote=True, is_published=False)
]

# Print JobPost processing state
print(f"JobPosts being processed: {JobPost.objects.count()}")
for jobpost in JobPost.objects.all():
    print(f"Job: {jobpost.title}, Author: {jobpost.author.user.username}")
    print(f"Location: {jobpost.location.city}, Remote: {jobpost.is_remote}, Published: {jobpost.is_published}")
    print(f"Skills: {[skill.name for skill in jobpost.required_skills.all()]}")
    print(f"Salary: ${jobpost.salary_min} - ${jobpost.salary_max}")
checkpoint created: anihpj_worker:jobpost_processing_state CHECKPOINT NAME CREATED jobpost_processing_state just now JobPosts being processed: 3 Job: Senior Django Developer, Author: john_doe Location: San Francisco, Remote: True, Published: True Skills: ['Python', 'Django', 'PostgreSQL'] Salary: $120000 - $150000 Job: Python Data Scientist, Author: jane_smith Location: New York, Remote: False, Published: True Skills: ['Python'] Salary: $100000 - $130000 Job: Full Stack Developer, Author: john_doe Location: Remote, Remote: True, Published: False Skills: ['Python', 'Django'] Salary: $90000 - $120000

docker checkpoint ls

Official Docs: docker checkpoint ls | Docker CLI: docker checkpoint ls

List all checkpoints created for a specific container.

Full Description

The docker checkpoint ls command lists all checkpoints created for a specific container. For your anihpj Django project, this command helps you view all saved states for your Django development containers, track checkpoint history, and identify available restore points for debugging or development rollback scenarios.

When listing checkpoints for your anihpj Django application containers, the command displays checkpoint names, creation timestamps, and associated metadata. This enables effective management of development snapshots, debugging states, and testing restore points for your jobpost app components including Skills model states, User authentication checkpoints, Author profile snapshots, Location data backups, and JobPost processing states.

Syntax

# Basic checkpoint listing
docker checkpoint ls {container}

# List with custom format
docker checkpoint ls --format {template} {container}

# List checkpoints for Django web container
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_web

# List checkpoints with formatted output
docker checkpoint ls --format "table {{.Name}}\t{{.Created}}" anihpj_web

# List database container checkpoints
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_db

Parameters Description

  • container: Name or ID of the container to list checkpoints for. Must be existing container that has had checkpoints created (anihpj_web, anihpj_db, anihpj_worker).
  • --format: Format the output using a Go template. Supports table, json, and custom formats with fields like Name, Created, and Size for checkpoint information.

Attributes

  • Checkpoint Name: Unique identifier for each checkpoint. Shows descriptive names like 'debug_session', 'pre_migration', or 'feature_test_state'.
  • Creation Date: Timestamp when checkpoint was created. Helps identify most recent or relevant checkpoints for Django development workflows.
  • Checkpoint Size: Disk space consumed by checkpoint data. Important for monitoring storage usage in Django development environments.
  • Container State: Status of container when checkpoint was created. Indicates if checkpoint captured running or stopped container state.
  • Checkpoint Count: Total number of checkpoints for the container. Helps track checkpoint accumulation and storage management needs.

Methods

List with Custom Format

Syntax: docker checkpoint ls --format {template} {container}

Parameters: template (string) - Go template format string, container (string) - Django container name to list checkpoints for

Display checkpoint information in custom format for scripting, monitoring, or integration with Django development tools and CI/CD pipelines.

List for Development History

Syntax: docker checkpoint ls {django-container}

Parameters: django-container (string) - Django application container with development checkpoints

View complete checkpoint history for Django development containers, enabling tracking of development states and debugging sessions.

Return Value

Returns formatted list of checkpoints with names, creation dates, and metadata. On failure, returns error details about container access or listing issues.

  • Success (0): Checkpoint list displayed with specified format
  • Error (1): Container not found or no checkpoints exist
  • Error (125): Invalid command usage or Docker daemon error

5 Practical Django Examples

Example 1: Skills Model Development History (Checkpoint Tracking)

Scenario: Listing all checkpoints created during Skills model feature development to track development progress.

# List all checkpoints for Django web container
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_web

# List with detailed format
docker checkpoint ls --format "table {{.Name}}\t{{.Created}}\t{{.Size}}" anihpj_web

Django Models Context:

# Skills model development states preserved in checkpoints
class Skills(models.Model):
    name = models.CharField(max_length=100)
    category = models.CharField(max_length=50)
    level = models.CharField(max_length=20)
    description = models.TextField(blank=True)
    created_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    updated_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now=True)
    
    def __str__(self):
        return f"{self.name} - {self.level}"

# Skills data evolution tracked through checkpoints
checkpoint_skills_data = {
    'initial_state': [
        Skills(name="Python", category="Programming", level="Expert"),
        Skills(name="Django", category="Framework", level="Advanced")
    ],
    'feature_development': [
        Skills(name="PostgreSQL", category="Database", level="Intermediate"),
        Skills(name="Redis", category="Caching", level="Beginner")
    ],
    'final_state': [
        Skills(name="Docker", category="DevOps", level="Advanced"),
        Skills(name="Kubernetes", category="Orchestration", level="Intermediate")
    ]
}

# Print Skills development history from checkpoints
print("Skills Model Development History:")
print(f"Total Skills across all checkpoints: {sum(len(skills) for skills in checkpoint_skills_data.values())}")
for checkpoint_name, skills_list in checkpoint_skills_data.items():
    print(f"\nCheckpoint: {checkpoint_name}")
    for skill in skills_list:
        print(f"  - {skill.name}: {skill.category} ({skill.level})")

Django Models Context:

# User model evolution captured in database checkpoints
class User(models.Model):
    username = models.CharField(max_length=150, unique=True)
    email = models.EmailField()
    first_name = models.CharField(max_length=50)
    last_name = models.CharField(max_length=50)
    password_hash = models.CharField(max_length=128)
    is_active = models.BooleanField(default=True)
    is_staff = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    is_superuser = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    last_login = models.DateTimeField(null=True, blank=True)
    date_joined = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    
    def __str__(self):
        return self.username

# User data states preserved in migration checkpoints
migration_user_data = {
    'pre_email_migration': [
        User(username="john_doe", first_name="John", last_name="Doe", is_active=True),
        User(username="jane_smith", first_name="Jane", last_name="Smith", is_active=True)
    ],
    'post_email_migration': [
        User(username="john_doe", email="john@anihpj.com", first_name="John", last_name="Doe"),
        User(username="jane_smith", email="jane@anihpj.com", first_name="Jane", last_name="Smith")
    ],
    'authentication_update': [
        User(username="admin_user", email="admin@anihpj.com", is_staff=True, is_superuser=True),
        User(username="test_user", email="test@anihpj.com", is_active=False)
    ]
}

# Print User migration checkpoint analysis
print("User Authentication Migration History:")
print(f"Total migration checkpoints: {len(migration_user_data)}")
for checkpoint_name, users_list in migration_user_data.items():
    print(f"\nMigration Checkpoint: {checkpoint_name}")
    print(f"Users count: {len(users_list)}")
    for user in users_list:
        print(f"  - {user.username}: {user.email or 'No email'} (Active: {user.is_active})")

Django Models Context:

# Author model development tracked through feature checkpoints
class Author(models.Model):
    user = models.OneToOneField(User, on_delete=models.CASCADE)
    bio = models.TextField(max_length=500, blank=True)
    profile_image = models.ImageField(upload_to='author_profiles/', null=True, blank=True)
    website = models.URLField(blank=True)
    github_username = models.CharField(max_length=39, blank=True)
    linkedin_profile = models.URLField(blank=True)
    specializations = models.ManyToManyField(Skills, blank=True)
    years_experience = models.PositiveIntegerField(default=0)
    is_verified = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    reputation_score = models.IntegerField(default=0)
    created_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    
    def __str__(self):
        return f"Author: {self.user.username}"

# Author development states in feature checkpoints
feature_author_data = {
    'author_basic_profile': [
        Author(user_id=1, bio="Django developer with 5 years experience", years_experience=5),
        Author(user_id=2, bio="Full-stack developer specializing in Python", years_experience=3)
    ],
    'author_social_features': [
        Author(user_id=1, website="https://johndoe.dev", github_username="john_doe_dev", 
               linkedin_profile="https://linkedin.com/in/johndoe", is_verified=True),
        Author(user_id=2, github_username="jane_smith_dev", is_verified=False)
    ],
    'author_reputation_system': [
        Author(user_id=1, reputation_score=150, is_verified=True),
        Author(user_id=2, reputation_score=85, is_verified=True),
        Author(user_id=3, reputation_score=45, is_verified=False)
    ]
}

# Print Author feature development checkpoint analysis
print("Author Profile Feature Development:")
print(f"Feature development checkpoints: {len(feature_author_data)}")
for checkpoint_name, authors_list in feature_author_data.items():
    print(f"\nFeature Checkpoint: {checkpoint_name}")
    print(f"Authors count: {len(authors_list)}")
    for author in authors_list:
        print(f"  - User ID {author.user_id}: Experience: {author.years_experience} years")
        print(f"    Bio: {author.bio[:50]}..." if author.bio else "    No bio")
        print(f"    Verified: {author.is_verified}, Reputation: {author.reputation_score}")
        if hasattr(author, 'website') and author.website:
            print(f"    Website: {author.website}")

class Location(models.Model):

Example 5: JobPost Processing Queue Checkpoints (Background Task Analysis)

Scenario: Listing Celery worker checkpoints to analyze JobPost processing states and background task queue management during high-load periods.

# List JobPost processing checkpoints
docker checkpoint ls anihpj_worker

# Detailed format for queue analysis
docker checkpoint ls --format "table {{.Name}}\t{{.Created}}\t{{.Size}}" anihpj_worker

Django Models Context:

# JobPost model processing states captured in worker checkpoints
class JobPost(models.Model):
    title = models.CharField(max_length=200)
    description = models.TextField()
    author = models.ForeignKey(Author, on_delete=models.CASCADE)
    location = models.ForeignKey(Location, on_delete=models.CASCADE)
    required_skills = models.ManyToManyField(Skills)
    salary_min = models.DecimalField(max_digits=10, decimal_places=2, null=True, blank=True)
    salary_max = models.DecimalField(max_digits=10, decimal_places=2, null=True, blank=True)
    employment_type = models.CharField(max_length=20, default='FULL_TIME')
    experience_level = models.CharField(max_length=20, default='MID_LEVEL')
    is_remote = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    is_published = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    is_featured = models.BooleanField(default=False)
    application_deadline = models.DateTimeField(null=True, blank=True)
    created_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now_add=True)
    updated_at = models.DateTimeField(auto_now=True)
    
    def __str__(self):
        return self.title

# JobPost processing queue states in worker checkpoints
queue_processing_data = {
    'jobpost_queue_initial': [
        JobPost(title="Senior Django Developer", description="Looking for experienced Django developer...",
                author_id=1, location_id=1, salary_min=120000, salary_max=150000,
                employment_type='FULL_TIME', experience_level='SENIOR', is_remote=True),
        JobPost(title="Python Data Scientist", description="Data scientist role with Python focus...",
                author_id=2, location_id=2, salary_min=100000, salary_max=130000,
                employment_type='FULL_TIME', experience_level='MID_LEVEL', is_remote=False)
    ],
    'jobpost_processing_heavy_load': [
        JobPost(title="Full Stack Developer", description="Full stack position with Django and React...",
                author_id=1, location_id=3, salary_min=90000, salary_max=120000,
                employment_type='FULL_TIME', experience_level='MID_LEVEL', is_remote=True, is_published=True),
        JobPost(title="DevOps Engineer", description="DevOps role with Docker and Kubernetes...",
                author_id=2, location_id=4, salary_min=110000, salary_max=140000,
                employment_type='FULL_TIME', experience_level='SENIOR', is_remote=True, is_featured=True),
        JobPost(title="Junior Python Developer", description="Entry-level Python development position...",
                author_id=1, location_id=5, salary_min=60000, salary_max=80000,
                employment_type='FULL_TIME', experience_level='JUNIOR', is_remote=False)
    ],
    'jobpost_queue_optimized': [
        JobPost(title="Machine Learning Engineer", description="ML engineer for AI-driven job matching...",
                author_id=2, location_id=6, salary_min=140000, salary_max=180000,
                employment_type='FULL_TIME', experience_level='SENIOR', is_remote=True, is_published=True, is_featured=True),
        JobPost(title="Contract Frontend Developer", description="Short-term React development contract...",
                author_id=1, location_id=1, salary_min=80000, salary_max=100000,
                employment_type='CONTRACT', experience_level='MID_LEVEL', is_remote=True, is_published=True)
    ]
}

# Print JobPost queue processing analysis
print("JobPost Processing Queue Analysis:")
print(f"Worker checkpoint states: {len(queue_processing_data)}")
total_jobs = sum(len(jobs) for jobs in queue_processing_data.values())
print(f"Total jobs processed: {total_jobs}")

for checkpoint_name, jobs_list in queue_processing_data.items():
    print(f"\nQueue State: {checkpoint_name}")
    print(f"Jobs in queue: {len(jobs_list)}")
    published_count = sum(1 for job in jobs_list if hasattr(job, 'is_published') and job.is_published)
    featured_count = sum(1 for job in jobs_list if hasattr(job, 'is_featured') and job.is_featured)
    remote_count = sum(1 for job in jobs_list if job.is_remote)
    
    print(f"Published: {published_count}, Featured: {featured_count}, Remote: {remote_count}")
    
    for job in jobs_list:
        print(f"  - {job.title}")
        print(f"    Salary: ${job.salary_min:,} - ${job.salary_max:,}")
        print(f"    Type: {job.employment_type}, Level: {job.experience_level}")
        print(f"    Remote: {job.is_remote}, Published: {getattr(job, 'is_published', False)}")
